Objection from Mu'tazila and Philosophers in al-Musayara
Imam Maydani cites al-Musayara presenting the Mu'tazila and philosophers' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection raised by the Mu'tazila and philosophers regarding human power and action, as presented by Kamal ibn al-Humam in al-Musayara.

Core Idea
Power must be effective, so humans must independently produce their own voluntary acts.

This passage introduces a formidable objection against the Sunni position on divine creation of human actions. The author cites Kamal ibn al-Humam's al-Musayara, where the Mu'tazila and philosophers argue that since Allah creates a power in humans that is essentially effective, humans must be the independent creators of their voluntary actions. This objection sets up the need for a response, which will follow later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once, noting the flow from the author's introductory remarks to the citation.
2
Identify the premises
Find the two key premises: (1) Allah creates a power in the servant, (2) this power is essentially effective (ta'thir).
The distinction between voluntary movement and involuntary tremor is evidence of created power.
3
Understand the conclusion
The objection concludes that general Quranic texts must be specified to exclude human voluntary actions, making humans independent in producing their actions.
4
Note the nuance between Mu'tazila and philosophers
Both agree on independence, but differ: Mu'tazila say Allah creates the power by choice; philosophers say it's by necessary emanation when preparedness is complete.
5
Reflect on why this is a challenge
Consider why this objection threatens the Sunni doctrine that Allah creates all actions, including human voluntary acts.
The answer will be provided in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه لما كان هذا المقام مما تحيرت فيه أفهام الأعلام حتى أقر بعضهم بالعجز عن فهم المرام وتحقيقه يحتاج إلى مزيد كلام، وكان ممن جال في ذلك وحامى وناضل فيه كل علامة إمام، حتى ظهر الحق وقام وجمع بين المنقول والمعقول للأنام، صاحب المسايرة المحقق الكمال بن الهمام فسنح لي أن أذكر عبارته بالكمال والتمام؛ لما اشتملت عليه من الفوائد العظام. قال في المسايرة : فإن قيل : لا شك أنه تعالى خلق للعبد قدرة على الأفعال ولذا يدرك تفرقة ضرورية بين الحركة المقدورة والرعدة الضرورية، والقدرة ليست خاصيتها إلا التأثير فوجب تخصيص عمومات النصوص بما سوى أفعال العباد الاختيارية؛ فيكونون مستقلين بإيجاد أفعالهم بقدرتهم الحادثة بخلق الله تعالى إياها كما هو رأي المعتزلة والفلاسفة بلا فرق؛ غير أن قدرة العبد حادثة بإيجاد الله تعالى باختياره عند المعتزلة، وبطريق الإيجاب عند تمام الاستعداد
And know that this station is one that perplexed the understandings of the eminent, such that some admitted inability to grasp the intended meaning, and its verification requires more speech. Among those who roamed in this area, defended, and argued in it, every scholar and imam, until the truth became manifest and stood firm, and he combined the transmitted and the rational for the people, is the author of al-Musayara, the investigator Kamal ibn al-Humam. So it occurred to me to mention his statement completely and perfectly, because of the great benefits it contains. He said in al-Musayara: 'If it is said: There is no doubt that He, exalted, created power in the servant for actions, and therefore he perceives an essential distinction between the voluntary movement and the involuntary tremor. And power (qudra) has no characteristic except effect (ta'thir). So it is necessary to specify the general texts (of the Quran) to other than the voluntary actions of the servants, so that they become independent in bringing about their actions by their created power, which Allah created in them, just as is the view of the Mu'tazila and the philosophers without distinction, except that the servant's power is created by Allah's origination by His choice according to the Mu'tazila, and by way of necessitation (ijab) when the preparedness is complete.'
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage introduces a citation from al-Musayara by Kamal ibn al-Humam, a famous Hanafi scholar. The author acknowledges the difficulty of this topic. He then presents the objection of the Mu'tazila and philosophers: since Allah creates a power in the servant that is distinguished from involuntary tremor, and since power is essentially effective (ta'thir), then the general Quranic verses (e.g., 'Allah is the Creator of everything') should be specified to exclude voluntary human acts, meaning that humans independently bring about their own actions using the power Allah created in them. The only difference between the Mu'tazila and the philosophers is that the former consider the created power to be by Allah's choice, while the latter consider it by necessary emanation (ijab) upon complete preparedness. This objection is left unanswered here, to be resolved later.

Key Terms
المسايرة (al-Musayara)
الكمال بن الهمام (Kamal ibn al-Humam)
التأثير (ta'thir)
الإيجاب (ijab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the objection claims that humans create their own power, but it actually says Allah creates the power and humans use it to produce actions.
Correction: The objection states that Allah creates the power in the servant; humans are independent in producing actions using that created power, not in creating the power itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection presented from al-Musayara?
A) That Allah creates all actions, and humans have no power.
B) That because Allah creates an effective power in humans, they must be independent in producing voluntary acts.
C) That power is not effective.
D) That humans create their own power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and philosophers argue that because power is inherently effective, humans must be the independent originators of their voluntary acts, challenging the Sunni view that Allah creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you truly feel that Allah is the sole creator of that action, or do you sense an independent ability within yourself? Which feeling does the Mu'tazila's argument appeal to, and why is that dangerous for your tawhid?
?
You often rely on your own power and effort in daily tasks. How does the claim that 'power has no characteristic except effect' subtly challenge your reliance on Allah? What would change in your life if you consistently remembered that only Allah's power creates effects?
?
The objection uses the natural distinction between voluntary movement and involuntary tremor to prove human independence. Think of a moment when you made a choice against your lower desires: did you attribute that success to your own power or to Allah's creation of that action? Be honest—how does this lesson expose the weakness of your current level of tawhid af'al?

Types of Human Movement: Compelled vs Voluntary and Taklif
The author distinguishes between compelled and voluntary movements and their relation to taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between compelled and voluntary movements and why only voluntary actions are subject to divine command and prohibition.

Core Idea
Only voluntary actions carry responsibility; compelled actions are beyond human capacity.

This passage from Imam al-Tahawi's creed introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic theology: human movements are either compelled or voluntary. The author explains that divine commands and prohibitions only apply to voluntary actions, because taklif requires the ability to choose. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion on human capacity and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation, noting the two types of movement described. Pay attention to how each is defined and what rulings apply.
The passage also includes an introduction to a later discussion citing Imam Kamal ibn al-Humam.
2
Identify the two movement types
The passage explicitly distinguishes between compelled movement (حركة جبرية) and voluntary movement (حركة اختيارية). Note the criteria for each.
Compelled is like a tremor; voluntary involves intention and capacity.
3
Understand the implications for taklif
Only voluntary movements are subject to command, prohibition, and taklif. Ask yourself why compelled actions are excluded.
The author says taklif is only for what the servant has power to do or refrain from.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنها كحركة المرتعش؛ فهذه محض مقدور الله تعالى خلقا وإيجادا، وتارة حركة اختيارية عند قصد العبد ويُقدره على صرفها إلى أي فعل شاء إلا أن الله يأمره بصرفها إلى الطاعة وينهاه عن صرفها إلى المعاصي؛ فكان تكليفا بما للعبد قدرة على الإيتاء به والامتناع عنه؛ ولهذا في الحركة الجبرية لم يرد الأمر بها والنهي عنها ولم يتعلق بها تكليف. واعلم أنه لما كان هذا المقام مما تحيرت فيه أفهام الأعلام حتى أقر بعضهم بالعجز عن فهم المرام وتحقيقه يحتاج إلى مزيد كلام، وكان ممن جال في ذلك وحامى وناضل فيه كل علامة إمام، حتى ظهر الحق وقام وجمع بين المنقول والمعقول للأنام، صاحب المسايرة المحقق الكمال بن الهمام فسنح لي أن أذكر عبارته بالكمال والتمام؛ لما اشتملت عليه من الفوائد العظام. قال في المسايرة :
... like the movement of a trembling person; this is purely the creation and existence of Allah. And sometimes a voluntary movement when the servant intends and He enables him to direct it to any action he wills, except that Allah commands him to direct it to obedience and forbids him from directing it to sins; thus it was a charge (taklif) regarding that which the servant has the ability to perform or refrain from. Therefore, with respect to compelled movement, there was no command or prohibition regarding it, nor was it subject to a charge. And know that since this station is one wherein the understandings of the eminent have become perplexed, such that some of them admitted incapacity to grasp the intended meaning, and its verification requires additional speech, and among those who discoursed on it, defended, and contended in it was every learned imam, until the truth appeared, stood firm, and combined the transmitted and the intellectual for the people, the author of al-Musayara, the verifier al-Kamal ibn al-Humann, so it occurred to me to mention his words completely and fully, because of the great benefits they contain. He said in al-Musayara: ...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a preliminary classification of human movements into two types: compelled (like a tremor) which are entirely created by Allah with no role for the servant, and voluntary movements which the servant intends and can direct. The author explains that only voluntary actions are subject to divine commands and prohibitions, since taklif (responsible obligation) requires the ability to perform or refrain. Compelled actions have no commands or prohibitions. Then the author notes the difficulty of this topic and introduces the work of Kamal ibn al-Humam (al-Musayara) as a reliable source for the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
كسب
مقدور
حركة اختيارية
حركة جبرية
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human capacity negates divine power or that compelled actions are also subject to reward and punishment.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes: compelled actions are purely divine creation with no human role, so no taklif; voluntary actions involve human capacity granted by Allah, and thus are subject to command and prohibition.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between compelled movement and divine command/prohibition?
A) Compelled movement is subject to command and prohibition but not reward.
B) Compelled movement is not subject to command or prohibition because the servant has no ability.
C) Compelled movement is forbidden.
D) Compelled movement is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Only voluntary actions are subject to divine command and taklif because they involve human capacity; compelled actions are purely divine and carry no responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you catch yourself thinking, 'I couldn't help it' or 'It was destined'? According to this lesson, those excuses apply only to compelled movements—not to your voluntary choices. Will you now take full ownership of every action you willingly commit, acknowledging that Allah enabled it and holds you accountable?
?
Many of us live as if our daily habits—like scrolling social media, choosing to pray or skip it—are neutral or beyond our control. But this lesson forces you to ask: are you treating your voluntary actions as 'compelled' to avoid responsibility? What would change if you saw each of your choices as a direct subject of Allah's command and your own capacity?
?
The great scholars admitted perplexity on this issue, yet they persisted until the truth became clear. Are you willing to sit with the discomfort of not fully understanding divine decree and human action, instead of settling for simplistic answers that let you off the hook? What is one area of your life where you have been avoiding this deeper reflection?

The Kasb Theory: Responding to the Objection on Human Capacity
Kamal ibn al-Humam presents the Mu'tazili/philosophical objection and answers with the kasb theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari kasb theory reconciles divine creation with human responsibility by distinguishing between capacity as creation and capacity as acquisition.

Core Idea
Human capacity relates to action as acquisition, not independent creation.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the Ash'ari position on human acts. If Allah creates human capacity, and capacity naturally produces effects, then humans must be independent creators. The answer—the theory of kasb—shows how capacity can relate to an action without inventing it, preserving both divine sovereignty and human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the first part of the passage (up to 'فبطل الأمر والنهي'). This is the objection from the Mu'tazila and philosophers. Understand their premise: capacity implies effect, so humans must be independent.
Notice they use the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movements as proof.
2
Identify the two key claims
The objection rests on two claims: (1) capacity's specific property is effect (ta'thir), and (2) if humans are not independent, it would be pure coercion (jabr), invalidating commands and prohibitions.
3
Examine the answer
Read the answer starting from 'فالجواب'. The answer redefines the relation: capacity relates to the action as acquisition (kasb), not as invention (ikhtira'). This avoids both independence and coercion.
Key phrase: 'ليس من ضرورة تعلق القدرة بالمقدور أن يكون بالاختراع'.
4
Recognize the analogy
The answer uses an analogy: Allah's power in pre-eternity related to the world without creating it then. This proves that a relation of power does not necessarily entail creation. Similarly, human capacity can relate to an action without inventing it.
The analogy shows that 'effect' is not the only mode of relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا شك أنه تعالى خلق للعبد قدرة على الأفعال ولذا يدرك تفرقة ضرورية بين الحركة المقدورة والرعدة الضرورية، والقدرة ليست خاصيتها إلا التأثير فوجب تخصيص عمومات النصوص بما سوى أفعال العباد الاختيارية؛ فيكونون مستقلين بإيجاد أفعالهم بقدرتهم الحادثة بخلق الله تعالى إياها كما هو رأي المعتزلة والفلاسفة بلا فرق؛ غير أن قدرة العبد حادثة بإيجاد الله تعالى باختياره عند المعتزلة، وبطريق الإيجاب عند تمام الاستعداد عند الفلاسفة وإلا كان جبرا محضا فيبطل الأمر والنهي. فالجواب أن الحركة مثلا كما أنها وصف للعبد و مخلوقة للرب لها نسبة إلى قدرة العبد فسميت باعتبار تلك النسبة كسبا، وليس من ضرورة تعلق القدرة بالمقدور أن يكون بالاختراع، إذ قدرة الله تعالى متعلقة في الأزل بالعالم ولم يحصل الاختراع بها إذ ذاك، وعند الاختراع تتعلق به نوعًا آخر من التعلق فبطل أن القدرة مختصة بإيجاد المقدور بها ولم يلزم الجبر المحض؛ إذ كانت الحركة متعلق قدرة العبد داخلة في اختياره، وهذا حاصل كلام الحجة.
For it is said: There is no doubt that Allah has created for the servant a capacity (qudra) for actions, and therefore he perceives an essential distinction between the voluntary movement and the necessary tremor, and capacity's specific property is none but effect (ta'thir), so it is necessary to specify the general texts (such as 'Allah is the Creator of all things') to exclude the voluntary actions of servants; thus they become independent in bringing about their actions by their originated capacity, with Allah creating it, as is the opinion of the Mu'tazila and philosophers without difference; except that the servant's capacity is originated by Allah's creation by His choice according to the Mu'tazila, and by way of necessity (ijab) upon completion of preparedness according to the philosophers, otherwise it would be pure coercion (jabr) and command and prohibition would be invalid. The answer: That motion, for example, just as it is an attribute of the servant and created by the Lord, has a relation to the servant's capacity, and by that relation it is called kasb (acquisition). It is not necessary that capacity's relation to its object be by way of invention (ikhtira'), for Allah's power is related to the world in pre-eternity without the invention occurring then, and at the time of invention it relates to it by another type of relation; therefore it is disproved that capacity is specific to bringing about its object, and pure coercion does not follow, because the motion is related to the servant's capacity and is within his choice. This is the gist of the argument.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam objection from the Mu'tazila and philosophers: since Allah creates human capacity, and capacity naturally produces its effect, then humans must be the independent creators of their own actions. This would seem to contradict Qur'anic verses that make Allah the Creator of all things. To avoid this contradiction, they argue those verses should be specified to exclude human voluntary actions. Otherwise, if humans are not independent, it would be coercion, making commands and prohibitions meaningless. The answer, drawn from Kamal ibn al-Humam, is the theory of kasb: motion has two aspects – it is created by Allah and is an attribute of the servant. The servant's capacity relates to it not as a creator but as an acquirer. This is analogous to Allah's power in pre-eternity: it relates to the world without causing existence then, and at the time of creation, a new relation occurs. So capacity is not necessarily creative. Thus, the action is within the servant's choice without being independently created, avoiding both pure coercion and independence. This is the essence of the argument.

Key Terms
قدرة
تأثير
إيجاد
كسب
اختراع
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The kasb theory is a mere semantic game that does not genuinely explain human responsibility.
Correction: The theory is based on a nuanced analysis: capacity can relate to an object in more than one way (invention vs. acquisition), and this is grounded in an analogy with Allah's own power.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the relation of capacity to its object not necessarily involve invention (ikhtira')?
A) Because capacity is powerless.
B) Because Allah's power related to the world in pre-eternity without creating it then.
C) Because the servant chooses independently.
D) Because the object is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The kasb theory resolves the tension between divine creation and human responsibility by distinguishing between the creative aspect (Allah) and the acquisitive aspect (human).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel a sense of pride as if you are the sole originator, or do you recognize that your capacity is merely an acquisition from Allah? Would acknowledging this change how you react to praise?
?
In moments of sin, do you ever blame fate or Allah's decree to avoid responsibility, or do you confront your own choice as the source of the action? How does the kasb theory call you to repent without despairing of Allah's mercy?
?
If all your actions are created by Allah but acquired by you, then what is left for you to boast about? Can you truly say 'I did this' without forgetting that the very ability came from Allah? How would your daily life change if you lived with constant awareness of this dependence?

The Second Objection to Kasb: Is It Just a Word?
An objector denies the validity of kasb as a distinct concept, equating it with creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the force of the second objection to the kasb theory and how the author begins to clarify the nature of the relation between capacity and action.

Core Idea
The objection claims 'kasb' is a meaningless term for creation; the response distinguishes the relation of capacity.

The second objection attacks the very notion of kasb, calling it a mere verbal trick. The opponent insists that acquisition (tahsīl) is identical to bringing into existence (ījād). The author begins his response by explaining that the relation of the originated capacity to its object is fundamentally different from that of divine power. This lesson presents the objection and the opening of the reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that 'kasb' has no distinct meaning; acquisition is creation. Understand the challenge.
2
Identify the key terms
Look at kasb, tahsīl, ījād, ta’thīr, qadra ḥāditha, irāda. These are the core concepts.
3
Analyze the author's initial response
The author begins by explaining the meaning of the relation of capacity to its object. Note that the response is cut off here.
The full answer continues in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعترض ذلك بقوله: «ولقائل أن يقول: قولكم: "إنها تتعلق بالمقدور لا على وجه التأثير فيها وهو الكسب" مجرد ألفاظ لم تحصلوا لها معنى، ونحن إنما نفهم من الكسب التحصيل، وتحصيل الفعل المعدوم ليس إلا إدخاله في الوجود وهو إيجاده، وقولكم: إن القدرة الحادثة تتعلق بلا تأثير كتعلق القدرة القديمة في الأزل، قلنا: معنى ذلك التعلق نسبة المعلوم [الوقوع ] من مقدوراتها إليها بأنها ستؤثر في إيجاده عند وقته؛ وذلك أن القدرة إنها تؤثر على وفق الإرادة وتعلق الإرادة بوقوع الشيء هو تخصيصه بوقته، والقدرة الحادثة يستحيل فيها ذلك؛
Then he objected to that, saying: 'One may say: Your statement "it relates to the object not by way of effect (ta'thir) and that is kasb" are mere words to which you have not given a meaning, for we understand from kasb nothing but acquisition (tahsil), and acquisition of a non-existent act is nothing but bringing it into existence, which is its creation (ijad). And your statement: "The originated capacity relates without effect, like the relation of the eternal power in pre-eternity" – we say: the meaning of that relation is the relation of the known occurrence from its potential acts to it, that it will effect its creation at its time. And that is because capacity effects according to will, and the will's relation to the occurrence of a thing is its specification to its time, and the originated capacity is impossible in that case...'
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection to the kasb theory. The objector claims that the distinction between effect (ta'thir) and kasb is merely verbal. In ordinary language, kasb (acquisition) means bringing something into existence, which is exactly creation. So the attempted distinction collapses. The objector further attacks the analogy with Allah's eternal power, arguing that the relation of power to its object is precisely that it will cause its existence at the appropriate time, and this is governed by will and specification. The answer begins but is cut off in this window: the author starts to explain that the meaning of the relation is different for human capacity. The lesson ends mid-answer, noting that the argument continues.

Defense of Kasb Against Determinism Objection
Defense of kasb against an objection linking it to jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari theory of kasb responds to the objection that it implies pure determinism and that the term 'kasb' is meaningless.

Core Idea
Kasb is a relational concept that avoids pure determinism without giving the servant creative power.

In this passage, the author defends the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) against a sharp objection. The objector claims that 'kasb' is an empty word, because true acquisition would mean bringing an act into existence, which is creation. The response argues that kasb is a relational attribution: the act is created by Allah but related to the servant's power, and this relation does not require the power to create. The passage also addresses the consequent problem of determinism, showing that without kasb, command and prohibition would become invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial response
Start by reading the first part of the source (from the beginning to 'وهذا حاصل كلام الحجة'). Notice how the author defines kasb as a relation between the act and the servant's power, without the servant creating the act.
The author uses motion as an example.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objection introduced by 'ولقائل أن يقول'. The objector insists that 'kasb' must mean bringing into existence (tahsil), which would make it identical to creation. This challenges the Ash'ari distinction.
The objector demands a meaningful definition.
3
Follow the rebuttal
Read the response to the objection (from 'قلنا: معنى ذلك التعلق...'). The author clarifies that the attachment of power is a relation to the known occurrence, and that originated power cannot be like eternal power in having a pre-eternal attachment.
Pay attention to the difference between eternal and originated power.
4
Grasp the final thrust
Read the conclusion from 'ولو سلم...' to the end. The author argues that even if the objection's definition were conceded, the real problem remains: without any effect from the servant's power, commands and prohibitions become meaningless, and a non-effectual attachment does not solve this.
This reinforces the necessity of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجواب أن الحركة مثلا كما أنها وصف للعبد و مخلوقة للرب لها نسبة إلى قدرة العبد فسميت باعتبار تلك النسبة كسبا، وليس من ضرورة تعلق القدرة بالمقدور أن يكون بالاختراع، إذ قدرة الله تعالى متعلقة في الأزل بالعالم ولم يحصل الاختراع بها إذ ذاك، وعند الاختراع تتعلق به نوعًا آخر من التعلق فبطل أن القدرة مختصة بإيجاد المقدور بها ولم يلزم الجبر المحض؛ إذ كانت الحركة متعلق قدرة العبد داخلة في اختياره، وهذا حاصل كلام الحجة. ثم اعترض ذلك بقوله: ولقائل أن يقول: قولكم: إنها تتعلق بالمقدور لا على وجه التأثير فيها وهو الكسب مجرد ألفاظ لم تحصلوا لها معنى، ونحن إنما نفهم من الكسب التحصيل، وتحصيل الفعل المعدوم ليس إلا إدخاله في الوجود وهو إيجاده، وقولكم: إن القدرة الحادثة تتعلق بلا تأثير كتعلق القدرة القديمة في الأزل، قلنا: معنى ذلك التعلق نسبة المعلوم الوقوع من مقدوراتها إليها بأنها ستؤثر في إيجاده عند وقته؛ وذلك أن القدرة إنها تؤثر على وفق الإرادة وتعلق الإرادة بوقوع الشيء هو تخصيصه بوقته، والقدرة الحادثة يستحيل فيها ذلك؛ لأنها مقارنة للفعل عندكم فلم يكن تعلقها إلا بالتأثير أو تبينوا له معنى محصلا ينظر فيه. ولو سلم فالمقتضي لوجوب تخصيص تلك النصوص بأفعال العباد لزوم الجبر المحض المستلزم لبطلان الأمر والنهي ولزومه على تقدير ألا تأثير لقدرة المكلف بالأمر والنهي، ولا يدفعه تعلق بلا تأثير.
The answer is that motion, for example, is a description of the servant and created by the Lord. It has a relation to the servant's power, so it is called, by virtue of that relation, 'acquisition' (kasb). It is not necessary that the attachment of power to the object of power be by way of invention (ikhtira'), since the power of Allah the Exalted is attached in pre-eternity to the world, and invention did not occur by it at that time, and at the time of invention it attaches to it with another type of attachment. Thus it is invalid that power is specialized for bringing into existence its object of power, and pure determinism (jabr) is not necessitated; since motion is the object of the servant's power and is included in his choice. This is the gist of the proof's speech. Then he objected to that by saying: 'One may say: Your statement that "it attaches to the object of power not in the mode of affecting it, which is acquisition," is mere words for which you have no meaning. We only understand from kasb 'acquisition' the meaning of attainment (tahsil), and attainment of a non-existent act is nothing but bringing it into existence, which is bringing it about (ijad). And your statement that the originated power attaches without effect, like the attachment of the eternal power in pre-eternity, we say: the meaning of that attachment is the relation of the known occurrence from its powers to it [i.e., the power] that it will affect its bringing into existence at its time. That is because power affects according to will, and the attachment of will to the occurrence of a thing is its specification by its time, but the originated power is impossible in that; because it is simultaneous with the act according to you, so its attachment is only by way of effect, or they must produce for him a meaningful meaning to be considered. Even if conceded, the ground for necessitating the specification of those texts [indicating] the actions of servants is the necessity of pure determinism, which entails the invalidity of command and prohibition, and its necessity upon the assumption that the power of the obligated person has no effect in the command and prohibition, and an attachment without effect does not repel it.'
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and defense of the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition). It begins by asserting that actions like motion are both descriptions of the servant and created by Allah, but they have a relation to the servant's power, hence called 'kasb'. The author argues that the attachment of power to an object does not necessarily involve creating it; divine power attaches in different ways. Then an objection is raised: the term 'kasb' is meaningless because 'acquisition' implies bringing into existence. The response clarifies that the attachment of power is about the relation of the known occurrence, and the servant's power is simultaneous with the act, so its attachment must be by effect, otherwise the argument from determinism persists. The author concludes that even if the kasb theory were accepted, the real problem remains: without any effect from the servant's power, command and prohibition would be invalid, and a non-effectual attachment does not solve this.

Correction: Kasb has a precise meaning: a relational attribution of an act to the servant's power without implying creation, as shown by the analogy with divine power's pre-eternal attachment.

Checkpoint

A) Kasb is a clear term with a single meaning.
B) The objector's own definition implies that kasb is identical to creation.

D) The objector's argument relies on a false analogy.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is not a meaningless term but a precise relational concept that allows human responsibility without compromising divine omnipotence, answering the objection that it implies pure determinism.

Dismissal of a Distinction between Creation and Created Act
Dismissal of an argument distinguishing creation from created act as irrelevant to human power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that human description with an act implies power over it is irrelevant in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Description does not imply control.

This passage tackles a subtle argument that tries to use language of description (e.g., calling a person 'moving') to prove that humans have power over their actions. The author shows that being described by an accident does not entail any choice or ability, because the accident is created by Allah. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the dense scholarly style.
The passage contains the opponent's argument inside quotation marks.
2
Identify the opponent's distinction
Find where the opponent distinguishes between creation (ijad) and the created thing (motion). Understand why they think this distinction supports human power.
They claim the servant can be described with motion because the created act subsists in him.
3
Understand the dismissal
The author calls the argument 'extraneous' (ajnabi). See how he explains that it only addresses description, not power or choice.
The example of whiteness in another being is key: the one who creates whiteness in a wall is not called white.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل: إيجاد الحركة غير الحركة؛ فالإيجاد فعل الله تعالى والموجود وهو الحركة فعل العبد وموصوف به حتى يشتق منه اسم المتحرك، وليس مشتقا للموجد اسم من متعلّق فعله؛ فلا يقال لموجد البياض في غيره أبيض بخلاف من قام به فأجنبي؛ إذ لا يتعرض إلا لكونه متصفًا بالعرض بعد إيجاد غيره إياه فيه، وهو لا يوجب دخوله تحت اختياره فضلا عن تعلق قدرته به.
And what was said: 'Bringing into existence (ijad) of motion is other than motion; so bringing into existence is the act of Allah the Exalted, and the existent which is motion is the act of the servant and described by it, such that the name 'moving' (mutaharrik) is derived from it, and the one who brings into existence is not given a name derived from the object of his act; so one does not say of the one who brings into existence whiteness in another that he is white, unlike the one in whom it subsists' — is extraneous; for it does not address anything except his being described by the accident after another's bringing it into existence in him, and that does not necessitate its entering under his choice, let alone the attachment of his power to it.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage dismisses a specific argument that attempts to separate the act of creation (ijad) from the created thing. The argument claimed that since motion is created by Allah but subsists in the servant, the servant can be described as moving, and derivation of names supports this. The author responds that this is irrelevant ('ajnabi) because it only addresses the fact that the servant is described by the accident after Allah creates it in him. This does not imply that the servant has any choice over it, nor that his power is attached to it. Thus the objection fails to establish human power.

Key Terms
إيجاد
موجود
موصوف
مشتق
عرض
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being described by an act (e.g., being called 'moving') proves that a person has power and choice over that act.
Correction: The passage clarifies that being described only indicates that the accident subsists in the person, not that they have any control over its creation or occurrence.

Checkpoint
Why is the argument that 'the servant is called moving because motion subsists in him' considered irrelevant in this passage?
A) Because motion is not an accident.
B) Because it only shows description, not power or choice.
C) Because the derivation of names is a linguistic matter.
D) Because the servant cannot be described by motion.

Key Takeaway
The argument that the servant is described by an act does not prove he has power or choice over it; only Allah has power over creation.

Affirming Both Divine and Human Power
Objection and answer reconciling divine and human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī response to the apparent conflict between divine omnipotence and human agency, and the role of necessary knowledge in affirming both.

Core Idea
Affirming both divine and human power is an act of faith, not contradiction.

This lesson tackles a classic theological puzzle: if everything comes directly from Allah, how can humans have real power over their actions? The passage presents the objection and the Ashʿarī answer—a direct affirmation of both truths based on necessary knowledge. It models a key principle: accepting revealed and self-evident truths even when the how eludes reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Note the structure: an objection (if it is said) followed by an answer (we affirm both).
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the two proofs cited.
2
Identify the two proofs
The objection mentions two proofs: (1) every existent originates from Allah's power directly, (2) the servant's power attaches to voluntary acts, known by necessary knowledge of the difference between voluntary and involuntary motion.
These seem contradictory. How can both be true?
3
Understand the answer
The author simply says 'we affirm both, even if the reality is not known.' This is the Ashʿarī position: accept both truths without needing to reconcile them conceptually.
This lesson teaches that some theological truths are held together by faith, not by dissolving the paradox.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and its concise answer. The objector points out that two proofs are established: first, that every existent originates from Allah's power directly, and second, that the servant's power attaches to his voluntary acts (as known by the distinction between voluntary and involuntary motion). These seem contradictory. The author's answer is simply to affirm both, even if the way they combine is not fully understood. This reflects the Ash'ari position of accepting both divine omnipotence and human responsibility without resolving the metaphysical mechanics.

The Necessity of Human Efficacy for Moral Obligation
The author refutes the claim that human power attaches without effect, showing it leads to compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity of real human efficacy for the validity of divine commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
Without human efficacy, divine commands become meaningless.

In this passage, the author presents a decisive argument against the position that human power attaches to actions without real effect. He shows that such a view leads to pure compulsion, which would nullify the very basis of moral obligation. This argument is crucial for establishing human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
Notice the first part challenges the opponent's definition of 'attachment' (talluq). The second part draws the consequence if no efficacy is conceded.
The author uses 'wa law sallima' to transition.
3
Reflect on the necessity of efficacy
Ask yourself why the author insists that attachment must imply effect. Consider what would happen to commands if humans had no real power.
This connects to the core idea that commands require agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنها مقارنة للفعل عندكم فلم يكن تعلقها إلا بالتأثير أو تبينوا له معنى محصلا ينظر فيه. ولو سلم فالمقتضي لوجوب تخصيص تلك النصوص بأفعال العباد لزوم الجبر المحض المستلزم لبطلان الأمر والنهي ولزومه على تقدير ألا تأثير لقدرة المكلف بالأمر والنهي، ولا يدفعه تعلق بلا تأثير.
Because it is compared to the action according to you, so its attachment would not be except as affecting, or you must produce for it a well-defined meaning to be considered. And if it is granted, then the basis for the necessity of specifying those texts regarding the actions of servants is the necessity of pure compulsion which entails the invalidity of command and prohibition, and its necessity upon the assumption that there is no effect for the power of the responsible person in command and prohibition, and attachment without effect does not repel it.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a previous claim. The author addresses an opponent (likely Ash'ari) who argues that human power attaches to actions without exercising real influence (ta'thir). The author first points out that such an attachment can only mean affecting (ta'thir), otherwise the opponent must provide a meaningful alternative definition. Then, even if this point is conceded, the consequence is that if human power has no real effect, then any general texts implying human responsibility would lead to pure compulsion (jabr), which nullifies the very basis of divine command and prohibition. Mere attribution of action without efficacy does not solve the problem.

Key Takeaway
Real human efficacy is rationally necessary for divine commands to be meaningful.

Refuting the Distinction Between God's Act and Human Act
The author dismisses the objection that creation differs from the created action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinction between God's creation of an action and the action itself does not imply human power over it.

Core Idea
Creation and the created thing are distinct, but that does not give humans causal power.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle objection: the act of creating motion is different from the motion itself, so perhaps humans can be attributed with the motion even though God creates it. The author shows this is irrelevant to the real issue of human power and choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the objection and the author's response.
The objection claims a distinction between creating (ijad) and the created (mawjud).
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues that motion created by God can still be the servant's act because the creation is different from the created thing. He uses the analogy of a person causing whiteness in another not being called white.
The analogy suggests that the attribute (motion) can belong to the subject even if another creates it.
3
Understand the author's dismissal
The author says the objection is irrelevant (ajnabi). It only addresses the accident inhering in a subject, not whether the subject has choice or power over it.
Mere inherence does not imply causative power.
4
Connect to the larger issue
This relates to the debate on human free will and God's creation of actions. The author reinforces that actions are created by God, and humans acquire them but do not create them.
This lesson clarifies a specific argument used by those who claim human power over actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل: إيجاد الحركة غير الحركة؛ فالإيجاد فعل الله تعالى والموجود وهو الحركة فعل العبد وموصوف به حتى يشتق منه اسم المتحرك، وليس مشتقا للموجد اسم من متعلّق فعله؛ فلا يقال لموجد البياض في غيره أبيض بخلاف من قام به فأجنبي؛ إذ لا يتعرض إلا لكونه متصفًا بالعرض بعد إيجاد غيره إياه فيه، وهو لا يوجب دخوله تحت اختياره فضلا عن تعلق قدرته به.
And what is said: 'The creation of motion is other than the motion; so creation is the act of Allah Almighty, and the created thing which is motion is the servant's act and he is described by it such that the name 'mover' is derived from it, and there is no name derived for the creator from the object of his act; thus one does not say of the creator of whiteness in another that he is white, unlike the one in whom it inheres' — this is irrelevant; because it only addresses his being qualified by the accident after another created it in him, and that does not necessitate its entering under his choice, let alone the attachment of his power to it.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector tries to distinguish between God's act of creating motion (ijad) and the resulting motion itself (mawjud), claiming that the motion can be attributed to the human even though God creates it, similar to how a person causing whiteness in another is not called white. The author dismisses this as irrelevant (ajnabi). He explains that the objection only shows that an accident (such as motion) inheres in the subject after another creates it; this does not prove the subject has any choice or power over it, which is the real issue.

موجود
حركة

موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing an accident to a person implies that person has power over it.
Correction: The author shows that mere inherence does not confer causative power; the human may be described by the action but does not create it.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the objection about 'creation of motion being other than motion' irrelevant?
A) Because it does not address whether the human has choice and power over the motion.
B) Because motion is not an accident.
C) Because God alone creates everything.
D) Because the objection is too technical.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between God's creation of an action and the action itself does not imply human causal power; the real issue is whether humans have choice and power over their actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Specifying General Premises to Avoid Compulsion
The author responds to an objection about rational proofs and divine causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why general rational premises in kalam can be specified and how this specification preserves human moral responsibility.

Core Idea
General premises must be specified to avoid implying pure compulsion.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument about the flexibility of rational proofs. An objector claims that established rational proofs force a contradiction between divine causation and human efficacy. The author responds by showing that these proofs are general premises that can and must be specified, and that the specifying factor is a rational necessity: otherwise, moral obligation would be nullified. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection carefully: rational proofs are claimed to require both direct divine creation and human efficacy, leading to an apparent contradiction.
The objector admits the necessary knowledge of human efficacy but claims it forces an irrational conclusion.
2
Identify the admission
Notice that the author first points out that the objector has admitted the necessary knowledge of human power attaching to voluntary acts.
This admission becomes a key premise.
3
Grasp the response about specification
The author argues that the rational proofs are general ('umumat) and can be specified (takhsis). If not specified, they lead to pure compulsion (jabr), which invalidates moral obligation (taklif).
The specifying factor is a rational necessity: avoiding the absurdity of nullifying obligation.
4
See the dismissal of other arguments
The author dismisses additional rational arguments as not binding, reiterating that the core issue is the efficacy of human power.
This reinforces the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: قام البرهان على وجوب كون كل موجود صادرًا عن قدرته تعالى ابتداء بلا واسطة وقام على وجوب تعلق قدرة العبد بأفعاله الاختيارية؛ للعلم الضروري بالتفرقة بين حركته صاعدًا وساقطًا فنقول بهما وإن لم تعلم حقيقة كيفية هذا التعلق فإنه غير لازم لنا. قلنا: حاصل هذا اعترافكم بأن العلم الضروري بتعلق قدرة العبد بحركته صاعدًا أمر ثابت، ثم ادعيتم أنه ألجأ إلى كونه على خلاف المعقول من معنى تعلق القدرة بمقدورها من كونه بلا تأثير وإيجاد لا ندري على أي وجه هو - ملجئ، وهو براهين وجوب استناد كل الحوادث إلى القدرة القديمة بالإيجاد وهو غير صحيح؛ فإن تلك البراهين إنما تلجئ لو لم تكن عمومات تحتمل التخصيص، فأما إذا كانت إياها ووجد ما يوجب التخصيص فلا. لكن الأمر كذلك وذلك المخصص أمر عقلي هو أن إرادة العموم فيها يستلزم الجبر المحض المستلزم لضياع التكليف وبطلان الأمر والنهي. وأما ما ذكروه من العقليات مما موضعه غير هذا المختصر فليس شيء منها لازما على ما يعلمه الواقف عليها بأدنى تأمل، ولو تم منها ما يلجئ إلى ما ذكر استلزم ما ذكرنا من بطلان التكليف، وقد قدمنا أن تعلق القدرة بلا تأثير لا يدفعه؛ لأن الموجب للجبر ليس سوى ألا تأثير لقدرة العبد في إيجاد فعله وهو باطل، وملزوم الباطل باطل ولهذا صرح جماعة من محققي المتأخرين من
For if it is said: The proof has been established for the necessity of every existent being originated from His power initially without intermediary, and it has been established for the necessity of the attachment of the servant's power to his voluntary actions, due to necessary knowledge of the distinction between his movement upward and downward, so we affirm both, even if the reality of the manner of this attachment is not known for it is not necessary for us. We say: The sum of this is your admission that the necessary knowledge of the attachment of the servant's power to his movement upward is established, then you claim that it forces [the conclusion] that it is contrary to rational meaning of the attachment of power to its power-able, being without effect and creation, we do not know in what manner it is — compelling. And it is proofs of the necessity of the dependence of all events on the eternal power for creation, and it is not correct; for those proofs only compel if there were not general [premises] that admit specification, but if they are general and there exists what necessitates specification, then not. But the matter is so, and that specifying factor is a rational matter, that intending generality in them necessitates pure compulsion which entails loss of obligation and nullification of command and prohibition. And as for what they mentioned of rational arguments which are beyond this summary, not one of them is binding as one who examines them with minimal reflection knows, and if any of them were to compel what is mentioned, it would entail what we mentioned of nullification of obligation, and we have already stated that attachment of power without effect does not repel it; because the cause of compulsion is nothing other than that the servant's power has no effect in creating his act, and that is false, and the concomitant of the false is false, and for this reason a group of later verifiers from
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a detailed response to an objection. The objector claims that rational proofs require both that every existent comes directly from God's power and that human power attaches to voluntary actions (based on necessary knowledge). The objector sees a contradiction. The author (the respondent) first notes that the objector admits the necessary knowledge of human efficacy. Then he critiques the objector's claim that these proofs force an irrational understanding of power attachment (ineffective attachment). The respondent argues that the rational proofs are general premises ('umumat) that can be specified (takhsis). The specifying factor is a rational necessity: if these premises are taken absolutely, they entail pure compulsion, which invalidates moral obligation. Therefore, the proofs must be specified, and the necessary knowledge of human efficacy is the specifier. The author further dismisses other rational arguments as not binding, and reiterates that the key point is that the cause of compulsion is the denial of human efficacy, which is false.

Refutation of the Claim of Unintelligible Attachment
The author refutes the opponent's claim that the proof forces an unintelligible attachment of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's claim that the proof forces an unintelligible mode of power attachment, using the concept of specification and highlighting the consequence of jabr.

Core Idea
General proofs do not compel when a rational specifying factor preserves obligation.

The opponent had argued that admitting necessary knowledge of the servant's power forces an unintelligible attachment—where power has no effect and we don't know how it attaches. The author counters this by showing that the opponent's proofs are not absolute; they are general premises that admit specification. The specification is rationally necessary to avoid pure predestination (jabr), which would nullify religious obligation (taklīf). This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how the author restates the opponent's claim before refuting it.
The passage is dense; read slowly and track the argument's flow.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and the author's response
Find where the author says 'the sum of this is that you have admitted...' and then 'then you claimed...' This sets up the refutation. The author's response begins with 'We say' and counters that the proofs are general and admit specification.
3
Understand the specifying factor
The specifying factor is rational: the general interpretation leads to pure jabr, which would invalidate taklīf and command/prohibition. This rational necessity forces specification.
This is the key move in the argument.
4
See the consequence for the opponent's position
The author argues that even if the opponent's rational arguments were decisive, they would lead to nullifying obligation—an absurdity. He also notes that later Ashʿari verifiers admitted that their position leads to jabr, with man compelled in appearance.
This shows the strength of the author's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: حاصل هذا اعترافكم بأن العلم الضروري بتعلق قدرة العبد بحركته صاعدًا أمر ثابت، ثم ادعيتم أنه ألجأ إلى كونه على خلاف المعقول من معنى تعلق القدرة بمقدورها من كونه بلا تأثير وإيجاد لا ندري على أي وجه هو ملجئ، وهو براهين وجوب استناد كل الحوادث إلى القدرة القديمة بالإيجاد وهو غير صحيح؛ فإن تلك البراهين إنما تلجئ لو لم تكن عمومات تحتمل التخصيص، فأما إذا كانت إياها ووجد ما يوجب التخصيص فلا. لكن الأمر كذلك وذلك المخصص أمر عقلي هو أن إرادة العموم فيها يستلزم الجبر المحض المستلزم لضياع التكليف وبطلان الأمر والنهي. وأما ما ذكروه من العقليات مما موضعه غير هذا المختصر فليس شيء منها لازما على ما يعلمه الواقف عليها بأدنى تأمل، ولو تم منها ما يلجئ إلى ما ذكر استلزم ما ذكرنا من بطلان التكليف، وقد قدمنا أن تعلق القدرة بلا تأثير لا يدفعه؛ لأن الموجب للجبر ليس سوى ألا تأثير لقدرة العبد في إيجاد فعله وهو باطل، وملزوم الباطل باطل. ولهذا صرح جماعة من محققي المتأخرين من الأشاعرة بأن مآل كلامهم هذا هو الجبر وأن الإنسان مضطر في صورة مختار.
We say: The sum of this is that you have admitted that necessary knowledge of the attachment of the servant's power to his upward movement is a fixed matter. Then you claimed that it forced [the conclusion] that it is contrary to the intelligible meaning of the attachment of power to its object (maqdur), in that it is without effect and without bringing into existence (ījād), and we do not know in what manner it is a compelling [argument] — and it is [based on] proofs of the necessity of every contingent thing's dependence on the eternal power for bringing into existence — and this is not correct. For those proofs would compel only if they were not general premises that admit specification. But when they are such, and there is found what necessitates specification, then [they do] not. But the matter is so, and that specifying factor is a rational one: that willing the generality in them entails pure predestination (jabr), which entails the nullification of obligation (taklīf) and the invalidation of command and prohibition. As for what they have mentioned of rational [arguments], the place of which is other than this short summary, not one of them is necessary, as anyone who examines them with the slightest reflection knows. And if any of them were complete so as to compel what was mentioned, it would entail what we mentioned of the invalidation of obligation. And we have already stated that the attachment of power without effect does not ward it off; because the cause of predestination is nothing other than that the servant's power have no effect in bringing his act into existence, and that is false, and the necessary concomitant of the false is false. For this reason, a group of later verifiers among the Ashʿaris declared that the outcome of their statement is predestination and that man is compelled in the guise of one who chooses.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's objection. The opponent had claimed that admitting necessary knowledge of the servant's power to his upward movement forces them to accept an unintelligible mode of attachment (that power has no effect and we don't know how it attaches). The author first restates the opponent's claim: 'You have admitted necessary knowledge... then you claimed it forces...' Then the author counters: The proofs that the opponent relies on (that all events must be created by God's eternal power) would only be necessary if they were absolute generalities that admit no specification. But they are generalities that do admit specification, and there is a rational specifying factor: the general interpretation that leads to pure predestination (jabr) would invalidate religious obligation (taklīf) and command/prohibition. Therefore, the specification is needed. The author then says the rational arguments the opponent brings are not decisive, and even if they were, they would lead to the absurd consequence of nullifying obligation. He reiterates that the attachment of power without effect (i.e., the Ashʿari position of kasb) does not escape the charge of predestination, because predestination results precisely from the power having no effect. Finally, he notes that later Ashʿari verifiers themselves admit that their position leads to jabr and that man is compelled in appearance only.

Key Terms

مقدور
جبر
تكليف

ملجئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that specifying general proofs is arbitrary or purely subjective.
Correction: The author shows that specification is rationally necessary to avoid the absurd consequence of jabr, which would invalidate religious obligation. Therefore, it is a principled, not arbitrary, specification.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the opponent's proofs do not necessarily compel the conclusion?
A) Because the proofs are not clear
B) Because the proofs are general and can be specified, and a specifying factor exists
C) Because the opponent has no proofs
D) Because the proofs are from revelation not reason

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the opponent by showing that their general proofs must be specified to avoid the rational necessity of jabr, which would nullify obligation; thus, the claim of unintelligible attachment fails.

This lesson shows that jabr nullifies obligation itself. Are you willing to examine your heart and see if you have hidden behind divine decree? Make a specific commitment to act on a duty you have been neglecting.
?
If later Ash'ari scholars themselves admitted that their position leads to compulsory compulsion (jabr), what does that say about your own intellectual comfort with difficult theological dilemmas? Are you willing to let go of easy answers and wrestle with the tension between divine power and human accountability? Commit to studying this issue further until you can articulate your own conviction.
?
This lesson argues that a rational specifying factor is necessary to preserve the reality of command and prohibition. Think of a command from Allah that you find difficult to obey. Do you truly believe you have the ability to choose to obey it, or has your heart accepted a subtle jabr that makes you feel resigned? Confess to Allah a specific instance where you acted as if compelled, and today choose to act with full awareness of your responsibility.
